<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c67bfaa0-9698-42c8-aab6-d94e52a33b44'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer4742744317608792660">Set in Los Angeles in the early 1980's, this coolly mesmerizing novel is a raw, powerful portrait of a lost generation who have experienced sex, drugs, and disaffection at too early an age, in a world shaped by casual nihilism, passivity, and too much money a place devoid of feeling or hope.<br/><br/>Clay comes home for Christmas vacation from his Eastern college and re-enters a lan</span>
  <span id="freeText4742744317608792660" style="display:none">Set in Los Angeles in the early 1980's, this coolly mesmerizing novel is a raw, powerful portrait of a lost generation who have experienced sex, drugs, and disaffection at too early an age, in a world shaped by casual nihilism, passivity, and too much money a place devoid of feeling or hope.<br /><br />Clay comes home for Christmas vacation from his Eastern college and re-enters a landscape of limitless privilege and absolute moral entropy, where everyone drives Porches, dines at Spago, and snorts mountains of cocaine. He tries to renew feelings for his girlfriend, Blair, and for his best friend from high school, Julian, who is careering into hustling and heroin. Clay's holiday turns into a dizzying spiral of desperation that takes him through the relentless parties in glitzy mansions, seedy bars, and underground rock clubs and also into the seamy world of L.A. after dark.</span>

<div id="review_19681473" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19681473" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419287-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289534648p2/419287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19681473?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/419287-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Trade Paperback " href="/book/show/12562487-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of today's mtv reality shows about overprivileged, lobotomized so-cal youth</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jessica by: </span>
      marshall
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=groups-of-people">groups-of-people</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=california-%C3%BCber-alles">california-über-alles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=substance-related-disorders">substance-related-disorders</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=leetle-boys">leetle-boys</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19681473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10100636102132781479">This book seems boring and shallow, and reading it gives me an anesthetized, hollow, detached feeling that I would not describe as entirely pleasant.<br/><br/>And yet I cannot seem to stop, and whenever I have to, I become very anxious to return to it as quickly as I can. Its appeal is no less powerful for being difficult to pinpoint or explain.<br/><br/>This experience reminds me of something, but I'm not sure what.... Oh yeah, I know: <i>Bright Lights, Big City</i>. Way better, though, so far. I love all the characters'</span>
  <span id="freeText10100636102132781479" style="display:none">This book seems boring and shallow, and reading it gives me an anesthetized, hollow, detached feeling that I would not describe as entirely pleasant.<br /><br />And yet I cannot seem to stop, and whenever I have to, I become very anxious to return to it as quickly as I can. Its appeal is no less powerful for being difficult to pinpoint or explain.<br /><br />This experience reminds me of something, but I'm not sure what.... Oh yeah, I know: <i>Bright Lights, Big City</i>. Way better, though, so far. I love all the characters' clothes.<br /><br />--------<br /><br />Okay, so I really, really liked this a <i>lot</i>, though I totally get why a lot of people didn't. I must say I find many reactions to it perplexing. The Village Voice blurb on the back of my copy calls <i>Less Than Zero</i> "sexy and sassy," which has to be one of the most bizarre characterizations imaginable: to me, this is one of least sassy, least sexy books I can think of (might tie with Marilynne Robinson's <i>Gilead</i> for that prize?). However, maybe that's just because I got confused and missed the point, as often happens.... I mean, a lot of what I kept thinking while reading this was about how tragically I was born in the wrong time, and why didn't <i>I</i> ever get to see Fear and X in LA in their heyday, and I'm pretty sure this was not really what I was supposed to take away from this novel.<br /><br />My experience of this book was no doubt colored by an unexpected plate-of-shrimp coincidence of life and fiction that I cannot expand upon adequately on this family website. I will say that I think this is the perfect cocaine novel because it so perfectly epitomizes the soul-sucking hollowness and numb angst at the core of this kind of lifestyle and drug use (or so I've heard).<br /><br />The reason why I thought this was so good, though, and what I'm surprised no one else on here seems to have felt, was that while in one way this was such a total period piece specifically criticizing the materialistic hedonism of the eighties or whatever, to me it transcended that. I seem to be in the minority in feeling this way, and without that sense that there was a larger point, this novel would've been just the cheap trick many other readers accuse it of being. To me, though, this completely deadening, unappealing, unglamorous litany of friends' names and routes driven and restaurants visited and drugs taken was so skillfully done because it should have been so boring but was somehow strangely mesmerizing. With a few missteps towards the end -- I found the whole thing with the friend and the pimp maudlin, silly, and totally off pitch from the rest of the novel -- everything is presented in a flat, deadpan way that makes it both so horrific and yet comprehensible. I never wondered why this kid was doing the things that he did, and that was where the book worked for me, because it's what created a kind of bridge to other lives, including my own.<br /><br />If <i>Less Than Zero</i>'s just a criticism of spoiled, zonked-out rich kids, there's not much of a point to this book, but if you start thinking about your own life, and life in general, then for me that's where it transcends its subject matter. You look at these extreme, exaggerated characters' ridiculous activities and the bizarre, soulless ways they live and relate to each other and it seems so sickening and meaningless, but then in a certain way it forces you to look at activities and life and relationships generally with a wider scope, and you start to wonder how meaningful any of it is, even if you aren't some gross millionaire LA cokehead, even if you're some mild-mannered social worker whose biggest addiction is Bookface. Like, this character's life is obviously pointless, but really, let's be honest, how much of a point is there to anything?<br /><br />Does that any make sense? It did to me. I know the point I'm saying he makes isn't particularly brilliant or earth-shaking, plus maybe I'm giving BEE too much credit, but I thought this book worked. If you look at it just as a satire of this kind of lifestyle then yeah, it seems like a waste of paper, because how tough a target are these subjects? But then if you start thinking about glass houses and stones, for me that's where it gets good. It's a certain nihilistic way of looking at the world that I usually try to shy away from myself, but it was good to be reminded of it, because this stuff is there. People are really like this. I mean, they are and they aren't, you know?<br /><br />Reading this book also reminded me of that time I went out on a date with my (formerly) Angeleno Bookster Marshall. When he finally came to New York, I was dismayed to learn that in fact he'd been joking about his willingness to breed with me, but after I got over that initial disappointment, we scored a gram and spent a very pleasant evening going up and down in the elevator of the Flatiron building, arguing over Elvis Costello and <i>American Psycho</i>, and gossiping about our mutual Booksters. It was a fun evening, and it's too bad Marshall wouldn't reproduce with me because I bet those Bookster genes would've created an awesome reviewer, albeit one with a frighteningly low birth weight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10100636102132781479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_115132601" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115132601" />
    <a title="Krok Zero" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1373169-krok-zero"><img alt="Krok Zero" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115132601?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krok Zero" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krok Zero" href="/user/show/1373169-krok-zero">Krok Zero</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1373169-krok-zero?shelf=summer-2010">summer-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115132601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer386411607491898200">Last year I spent a few months as an intern for a major national arts publication, which shall remain nameless because that makes me look cooler than if I just blurted it out. I had a few regular duties at this (unpaid) gig, the primary one being transcription of interviews. You might think that transcribing is drudgery, and in a sense it is. But if the interview subject was interesting—and, given this publication's bent and cachet, most of the subjects were interesting—it provided a rare glimps</span>
  <span id="freeText386411607491898200" style="display:none">Last year I spent a few months as an intern for a major national arts publication, which shall remain nameless because that makes me look cooler than if I just blurted it out. I had a few regular duties at this (unpaid) gig, the primary one being transcription of interviews. You might think that transcribing is drudgery, and in a sense it is. But if the interview subject was interesting—and, given this publication's bent and cachet, most of the subjects were interesting—it provided a rare glimpse into the messy vocal raw material of an interview, as opposed to the cleaned-up, translated-into-printed-words final product.<br /><br />One of the most fascinating interviews I transcribed was with none other than Bret Easton Ellis. The occasion of the interview was the release of the film version of Ellis' <i>The Informers</i>, which, according to Ellis and just about everyone else who saw it, was pretty much of a misbegotten failure. (Ellis co-wrote the script, but the film was apparently hacked to bits in the editing room; his tone toward the film was one of aggrievement, and he insisted that the longer, un-fucked-with cut of the film—it was supposed to be a sprawling, Altman-esque epic—was good. It's doubtful we'll ever see it. I'm actually not sure how much of this stuff made it into the printed interview, since some of it was supposed to be off the record.) Listening to the interview was an odd experience, because Ellis is an odd man. He was very personable and friendly toward the interviewer, moreso than any other subject I transcribed—he seemed to believe that he was just shooting the shit with this critic over the phone rather than giving an interview, and consequently he didn't seem to care much about staying on topic or saying things that made sense. As on his Twitter feed, he mostly talked about movies. Apparently a huge cinephile, Ellis kept prodding the interviewer with questions about which films he'd seen lately, what did he think of film X, how much he hated film Y, etc. My favorite moment went like this:<br /><br /><b>Interviewer:</b> {Thoughtful, penetrating question about Ellis' work}<br /><br /><b>Ellis:</b> {Loooooooooooooooong pause}<br /><br /><b>Ellis:</b> Did you see <i>Monsters vs. Aliens</i>?<br /><br />I shit you not, folks. I can't remember if Ellis eventually answered the question but I do know he went off on how much he loved <i>Monsters vs. Aliens</i> for a few minutes. And I loved him for that. But sometimes he was cogent and he said some smart, interesting shit—he went off an inspired riff about aesthetics vs. morality, and while he was ostensibly talking about the Irish-hunger-strike film <i>Hunger</i> his comments obviously applied to his own work. And he was really, really nice. Like, weirdly nice. He has this reputation for bad-boy nihilism or misogyny or whatever, but the guy I listened to seemed like way more of a mensch than, say, Jonathan "Fuck You" Franzen. Having never read a word by the man, I went home that day liking him.<br /><br />Just the other day, over a year after the events related above, I went back to the offices of the aforementioned major national arts publication to interview for a copy editor position. Afterwards, not feeling too great about how it went, I consoled myself by hanging out in the used bookstore around the corner, where I walked out with copies of <i>American Psycho</i> and <i>Less Than Zero</i>. In fact, the inspiration for this purchase was not so much a sense-memory recall of last year's Ellis transcript as it was the recent <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114412471" rel="nofollow noopener">GR review of <i>American Psycho</i> by Brian</a>. That review was totally badass, and made me want to give this controversial writer the old college try.<br /><br />So, <i>Less Than Zero</i>: 200 pages in the company of the overprivileged, morally vacuous sons and daughters of neglectful Hollywood royalty in the cocaine-addled 1980s. I loved it, man. It feels like an important book, and that Ellis was only 19 when he wrote it makes it at once more impressive (because the writing is so confident) and more authentically disturbing (because no matter how much Ellis protests that his shit isn't autobiographical, let's look at the facts: Ellis wrote this book as a teenager from L.A going to college on the east coast; the book is about a teenager from L.A. home from college on the east coast; and even if nothing that happens in the book specifically happened to him in real life, he was clearly doing what teachers tell you to do—he was writing what he knew. And what he knew wasn't pretty). So yeah, five stars; here's a few reasons why I'm all about this shit:<br /><br /><b>It's viscerally effective.</b> The vignette structure and clipped prose style propel the book along in a speedy, disorienting haze that mirrors protagonist Clay's fucked mental state. It moves, and if you wanted to just read this book in one quick burst of a sitting without really thinking about it at all you would probably still have a worthwhile experience. Like I said, visceral.<br /><br /><b>It's majorly evocative of time and place.</b> I'm sure you've heard that thing James Joyce said about how if Dublin burned down it could be rebuilt based on <i>Ulysses</i>. Well, if the dream architects from <i>Inception</i> wanted to recreate 1980s Los Angeles they would need a copy of <i>Less Than Zero</i> to use as a reference guide. I haven't felt so immersed in the '80s since I watched <i>Earth Girls Are Easy</i>, or so L.A.-ified since I read Chandler. So many references to Tab, Betamax, MTV and (of course) cocaine—and that's just what's on the surface.<br /><br /><b>It's deceptively complex.</b> There are interesting questions of form here. The novel is in the first person, but Clay's narration subverts our expectations about first-person narration, in that his flashes of introspection are few and far between; we know very little of his inner life (and we learn jackshit about other characters' inner lives). Instead, Clay's narration provides a just-the-facts-ma'am account of events that in a healthy person would provoke some kind of emotional reaction. On top of that is a fascinatingly discordant effect: we can tell that Clay is desperately miserable because it's reflected in the actions he relates, but he doesn't give us access to the thoughts and emotions by which we would typically understand his misery. By leaving this question mark, Ellis heroically refuses to supply facile answers about What's Wrong With The Kids These Days, letting us draw our own conclusions. Perhaps only a writer as young as Ellis was at the time could have been smart enough to do it this way. If he'd tried to fill in the blanks, to offer even the most poetic of explanations, the book would've been sunk by smarmy self-importance.<br /><br /><b>Underlying the horror is both a strain of dark humor and a stream of unexpectedly lovely grace notes.</b> This is an effect I associate specifically with the films of Harmony Korine—finding beauty in even the ugliest human environments. (Korine would be a great choice to adapt Ellis for the screen, though I doubt Harm would be interested in fucking with other people's work. The masked, murderous redneck freaks of <i>Trash Humpers</i> aren't so very different from Ellis' fucked-up Angeleno nihilists.) In <i>Less Than Zero</i> some of those grace notes can be found in the italicized interstitials recalling Clay's antediluvian trip to visit his grandparents; some are found in Ellis' physical descriptions of the L.A. landscape; and some pop up amidst the soulless anti-hedonism that makes up the bulk of the novel's action. As for humor, you have to squint a little bit to see it, but check out those scenes of Clay talking to his awful therapist, who just wants Clay to help him with his screenplay. Or the back-and-forth, gossipy inanities that some characters sling at each other about who slept with who, or the frequent refrain stating or asking if somebody O.D.'d. Hell, most of the book is funny if you look at it from a certain angle. And from a different angle it's a despairing tragedy.<br /><br />In the years since this novel was published I think the moneyed youth of America has gotten even more horrible, or at least equally horrible in different ways. Gary Shteyngart sort of tried to write about this in <i>Super Sad True Love Story</i>, but he failed. My generation needs its Bret Easton Ellis. And I need to read the rest of this guy's stuff.</span>
  <a data-text-id="386411607491898200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18316405481693315143" style="display:none">Rich kids doing drugs. And he was also a writer himself (published in Hustler!). He was in his 40's and still trying to break out. He had a son that was autistic and had tons of medical bills but because he still wanted to be a struggling artist his family had to suffer.<br />So, he gives me the manuscript of one of his books (that was rejected by several publishers because, as he stated, "It was too cutting edge").<br />It was is a super bad version of less that zero but really really raunchy and dirty and goobity gobbledy goo.<br /><br />He was also always quoting Dante's Inferno, but he only knew one line about all ye who enter here or whatever. <br />And he would always come into work an hour early and work off the clock so he could have everything already done before his shift started. That showed him.<br />Once, I was taking out the trash and he comes up and grabs it out of my hands and I was infuriated. I know it doesnt need to be said, but Robert Downey Jr. was really hot in that movie. I also kind of had a thing for James Spader. But not now, because he's kinda fat.<br />(Who am I kidding? I would still hit that.)<br /><br />P.P.S. You know what, Im not really sure I even read this book, or if I just think I did. Memory is deceiving.</span>

<div id="review_1767337341" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1767337341" />
    <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan"><img alt="Jr Bacdayan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552066595p2/16587936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1767337341?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jr Bacdayan" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan">Jr Bacdayan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1767337341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15380748013877306745">This novel irritated me but at the same time I couldn’t take my hands off it. I so clearly recognized the hardened apathy reflected in the eyes of Clay. He is a young man immobile, paralyzed by indecision, slowly rotting as he waits for whatever doom comes his way. His problem is not that he doesn’t know what he wants, but rather the ability to want has been lost in him. His circumstances, which usually is being driven by the person, is rather moving of its own accord, and he is aboard not steer</span>
  <span id="freeText15380748013877306745" style="display:none">This novel irritated me but at the same time I couldn’t take my hands off it. I so clearly recognized the hardened apathy reflected in the eyes of Clay. He is a young man immobile, paralyzed by indecision, slowly rotting as he waits for whatever doom comes his way. His problem is not that he doesn’t know what he wants, but rather the ability to want has been lost in him. His circumstances, which usually is being driven by the person, is rather moving of its own accord, and he is aboard not steering but watching indifferently as his fate is sealed without any resistance from him which could have prevented the crash he might be moving towards. It is a disease, this disregard, and it is terminal. He is alienated from himself, locked inside a wall of nothingness, and thus also unreachable to those around him. He is resigned to his life, a third person looking at his own body unconcerned with its wellbeing, going with the flow, only a voyeuristic sort of fulfillment in his gut. And as the days go by, he is disappearing more and more until the day that nothing remains and his invisibility is deemed permanent. <br /><br />Less Than Zero is the story of a university student who comes home and discovers that there never was such a thing as a home for him. Clay was born of a rich family in Los Angeles. His grandparents own hotel chains, his father is a big shot in Hollywood, he lives in a mansion in Beverly Hills, all his friends are high society people. It is apparent that he was raised in an environment that fostered dysfunction, an environment so enamored by its own wealth and glamor that it kills off any other function it has to offer. And so fresh off his freshman year from the relatively quiet place of New Hampshire, he goes home for summer vacation to kill time until classes start. Uncertain that there is anything else to do he goes on a psychedelic romp and engages in all sorts of debaucheries from snorting cocaine by the minute, gender indifferent acts of sexual deprivation, not to mention other sort of drugs, liquor, and all the twisted novelties Hollywood has to offer, moving from party to party in search for some unreachable form of satisfaction. He goes through all this in a kind of painful stupor trying to feel something, anything while balancing his rocky relationship with his girlfriend Blair, and trying to make sense of his best friend Julian. Amidst all that is happening there lingers a dreadful clarity, a soberness that can only be found as one dwells in the most chaotic of places, like a man in the middle of a bacchanal, realizing that he feels awful. <br /><br />A lot of people will read this novel and hate it. A few, however, will sense an unsettling familiarity in its hollow pages. There needs to be a certain disposition for someone to truly appreciate this novel, a disposition so readily seen in the addled millennial. Those who can understand Clay when he says "Nothing. Nothing makes me happy. I like nothing." Because as a book, this is infected with the same disease that Clay is suffering from, an emptiness seeping away the lifeblood of its unfocused words. This novel doesn’t know what it wants. It reminds me of a certain passage from its body that goes:<br /><br />“But this road doesn't go anywhere,” I told him.<br />“That doesn't matter.”<br />“What does?” I asked, after a little while.<br />“Just that we're on it, dude,” he said.”<br /><br />There is no other purpose to this story. It moves resigned to its writing with no higher aim or message. Ellis, then a 21 year-old, writes his work and in his prose he injects a virus that permeates an enigmatic lifelessness, a wayward languor that eats away the vital soul.<br /><br />In the end, in order to not feel depressed, I shall borrow a line from Yukio Mishima, something I read I few days ago that pierced me deeply. <br /><br />“I dream of a moment when, without my asking, my actions will betray completely this part of me that asks for nothing.”<br /><br />Maybe Clay, in one of his reveries, had this thought. Maybe you’ve had it too. Maybe then it’s not too late for us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15380748013877306745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2517593174" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2517593174" />
    <a title="Whitney Atkinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14457130-whitney-atkinson"><img alt="Whitney Atkinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550868411p2/14457130.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2517593174?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whitney Atkinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whitney Atkinson" href="/user/show/14457130-whitney-atkinson">Whitney Atkinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2517593174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2319647345703007848">TW: drug abuse, pedophilia<br/><br/>The person who recommended this to me cited it as her favorite book of all time, but she had read it for a class, so I think we had different experiences with it. This book is steeped in melancholy and nihilism, which I typically enjoy, but the format and emotionlessness of this often made it difficult to read, so it took me over a month to complete. Still, I enjoyed its themes and totally understand why my friend connected with it so much. I'm jealous that she got to d</span>
  <span id="freeText2319647345703007848" style="display:none">TW: drug abuse, pedophilia<br /><br />The person who recommended this to me cited it as her favorite book of all time, but she had read it for a class, so I think we had different experiences with it. This book is steeped in melancholy and nihilism, which I typically enjoy, but the format and emotionlessness of this often made it difficult to read, so it took me over a month to complete. Still, I enjoyed its themes and totally understand why my friend connected with it so much. I'm jealous that she got to discuss it in a class because I think I would have gotten more out of it that I didn't even realize. This is one of those books that looks simple on the surface but is packed with so much meaning and intricately laced themes. It's definitely not for the lighthearted, though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2319647345703007848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_130575636" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130575636" />
    <a title="Kathryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1497350-kathryn"><img alt="Kathryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250569584p2/1497350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/130575636?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/1497350-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=family-drama">family-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=drugs">drugs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130575636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12400181537812166393">Books of this nature age well with me. I keep thinking about what happened, what Ellis might have meant. I find it fascinating what people walk away with from this and American Psycho. It seems rather obvious to me that this book is not just about spoiled rich drug addicts wasting away while taking some of their world with them. The characters' actions, more specifically their lack of action, says so much for the state of the times in this book, for LA, for American culture, all of which I find </span>
  <span id="freeText12400181537812166393" style="display:none">Books of this nature age well with me. I keep thinking about what happened, what Ellis might have meant. I find it fascinating what people walk away with from this and American Psycho. It seems rather obvious to me that this book is not just about spoiled rich drug addicts wasting away while taking some of their world with them. The characters' actions, more specifically their lack of action, says so much for the state of the times in this book, for LA, for American culture, all of which I find maybe even more relevant today. <br /><br />The fact that people can walk away from this book thinking it is only about drugs, that they "did not get it", or they thought it pointless, just makes me sad. Ellis is an extremely talented writer. His writing is deceptive and layered. The best way for me to convey why I so far love his books is to relate what I think when I read them. I wonder what his characters say about me, why I am able to relate and what my reactions to what happens reflect about me. About LA (a place I hope never to return to) and other such cities, with sterile environments and filthy underbellies, places where people come together to consume and waste. About the indifference of youth and American culture. About relationships and detatchment, self abuse and self worth, vanity and denial. What Ellis writes feels real, no matter how awful or horrific. That is truly frightening. <br /><br />This book is especially disturbing to me because the youth protrayed are now everywhere, no longer only the rich or confined to the cities. Indifference spreads with each generation.<br /><br />The main character has zero emotional attachments to his world, his family and friends. He has desensitized himself into acceptance. When he faces things in the book that shock or bother him, his true self shines through. <br /><br />Three scenes really shocked me and that is all I will say about that since I did not see them coming at all.<br /><br />Some of the most disturbing parts of this book are simple comments made by Clay's family. Family should protect and it is little wonder how Clay started on his current path. Clay and his friends are at that age when they are becoming adults and are now responsible for their actions more than ever before. I am a firm believer that people instinctively know basic rights from wrongs and when a youth becomes an adult and chooses a knowing wrong, then they may be close to evil. Ellis' characters choose evil and let the guilt run off instead of settling in. <br /> <br />I think the book tried a little too hard towards the end but it worked so I bumped 4 stars to 5 stars. The typical use of brand names, music, and posh scenes, cities and clubs and film and music, all shine through as in American Psycho, though maybe not as strong here . This is an incredibly sad book about, as one character describes, a beautiful boy who makes no effort, and though I am having problems writing coherently, summing up my thoughts and reactions in an organized manner, I know I could ramble all night about this book and still be slightly confused as to why I like it so much. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12400181537812166393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3840658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3840658" />
    <a title="Derek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/239943-derek"><img alt="Derek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456073337p2/239943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3840658?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derek" href="/user/show/239943-derek">Derek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3840658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7621064647414430803">Why should I care about Bret Easton Ellis' characters if he doesn't care about them? The aptly titled <i>Less Than Zero</i> didn't bother to go into the character's inner-dialogue any more than it bothered to show a character that anyone might care about. Sure, the things they do (random sex, drug abuse, etc) make great fodder for fiction, but if there's no counterweight of compassion, what do I care if they fuck up their lives?<br/><br/>I get it: they're emotionally vacant and aimless because of the environment</span>
  <span id="freeText7621064647414430803" style="display:none">Why should I care about Bret Easton Ellis' characters if he doesn't care about them? The aptly titled <i>Less Than Zero</i> didn't bother to go into the character's inner-dialogue any more than it bothered to show a character that anyone might care about. Sure, the things they do (random sex, drug abuse, etc) make great fodder for fiction, but if there's no counterweight of compassion, what do I care if they fuck up their lives?<br /><br />I get it: they're emotionally vacant and aimless because of the environment they grew up in, and that is reflected by the deadpan delivery of the writing. I get the point, over and over again. And then he goes ahead and makes the point again. But I'm not interested in emotionally vacant writing. Sex slaves, underage girls, cheating, cocaine abuse, and crime: it can only carry itself so far, and the shock value wears off quickly. I was taught in a fiction class that emotionally numb characters are not interesting ones; I wish someone would've shared this with the emotionally numb, 19-year old Ellis when he wrote this book from the comfort of his Bennington dorm room.<br /><br />I have a hard time understanding how Ellis was put in the same high regard (the so-called "literary brat pack") as his brilliant friend, Jay McInerney. <i>Bright Lights, Big City</i> portrayed the same type of disillusioned young adults in an infinitely more satisfying and emotionally involving way. This one-trick pony only scores points for X and Elvis Costello references. Maybe the Fashion Network can film an adaptation so that the story can reach the target-market it tries so desperately to criticize.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7621064647414430803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3024659477?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pedro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pedro" href="/user/show/73200741-pedro">Pedro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11778262-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73200741-pedro?shelf=sweet-nightmares-and-awful-dreams">sweet-nightmares-and-awful-dreams</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3024659477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1196948363302380212">I read this book with the constant eerie feeling that I was reading someone else’s diary. I wanted to stop but couldn’t! And trust me, this was proper scary stuff. <br/><br/>How simple it is to stop caring or not to be afraid to lose if you think there’s actually nothing left to lose. How easy it is to think you can replace affection with a credit card. How so much easier it is to let yourself go when you’re young enough to think you’re going to live forever. <br/><br/>There’s a reason why this book became a bestse</span>
  <span id="freeText1196948363302380212" style="display:none">I read this book with the constant eerie feeling that I was reading someone else’s diary. I wanted to stop but couldn’t! And trust me, this was proper scary stuff. <br /><br />How simple it is to stop caring or not to be afraid to lose if you think there’s actually nothing left to lose. How easy it is to think you can replace affection with a credit card. How so much easier it is to let yourself go when you’re young enough to think you’re going to live forever. <br /><br />There’s a reason why this book became a bestseller and a classic, and there’s also a reason why a lot of readers would put it aside and claim that “Nah, this is not literature” or “Wtf, nothing really happens in this story”. Fair enough. We all are allowed to have our opinions and it’s good to have one of your own, but believe me, all aspects of human life are here between these pages. You’ll just have to want to see it. You can’t look away.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1196948363302380212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5494261801634547787">Reading this book is almost a painful exercise. Everyone is drugged up, f*@$ed up and nobody cares about anything other than getting high and wasted. Everyone is literally sleeping with everyone. Many meaningless sexual encounters where morals are left by the highway. The 80's were really about living the excess lifestyle and no place more than L.A where this book centres around. The book is one painfully awful situation after another, a lot of aimless wanderings, with lots of bad pointless dial</span>
  <span id="freeText5494261801634547787" style="display:none">Reading this book is almost a painful exercise. Everyone is drugged up, f*@$ed up and nobody cares about anything other than getting high and wasted. Everyone is literally sleeping with everyone. Many meaningless sexual encounters where morals are left by the highway. The 80's were really about living the excess lifestyle and no place more than L.A where this book centres around. The book is one painfully awful situation after another, a lot of aimless wanderings, with lots of bad pointless dialogue. Nihilistic youths with too much money, too much time and hormones is always a cocktail for unhealthy choices. Despite the seeming negatives I didn't completely hate this book, if that was it's intention to shock and display the vulgarities and vapidness of youth of the 80's then this delivers it's purpose. </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review99037307">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1228044828" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1228044828" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1228044828?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781455815241" href="/book/show/11008616-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1228044828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8321917711274708951"><i>Less Than Zero</i> is an affecting ridealong in a car full of coke-addled rich kids. The ending is properly shocking. I was, as was intended, thoroughly disgusted, as I'm sure you will be too. <br/><br/>I didn't like a single character. The book has all the appeal of a trainwreck that causes a chemical spill at your local kindergarten. You don't read this book for fun. You read it to justify your hatred of humanity and all things wealthy. <br/><br/>Christian Rummel does a fantastic job with the audiobook. <br/><br/>In summation:</span>
  <span id="freeText8321917711274708951" style="display:none"><i>Less Than Zero</i> is an affecting ridealong in a car full of coke-addled rich kids. The ending is properly shocking. I was, as was intended, thoroughly disgusted, as I'm sure you will be too. <br /><br />I didn't like a single character. The book has all the appeal of a trainwreck that causes a chemical spill at your local kindergarten. You don't read this book for fun. You read it to justify your hatred of humanity and all things wealthy. <br /><br />Christian Rummel does a fantastic job with the audiobook. <br /><br />In summation: The stars are for the writing and the fact that the book accomplishes what it sets out to do. It means to sicken, and it does. *slow clap*<br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: Kill 'em all and let Tom Cruise sort them out.</b><br /></span>

          </span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111887831-shine-story?shelf=2020">2020</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3244835078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16730812786304368064"><b>Honestly, I didn't like it. There were very few redeeming qualities, and I am ready to die on this hill.</b><br/><br/>For a while, I was going to leave this review blank and pretend that I was more neutral about it. I was worried that people would tell me that I "didn't get" the book, or the messages that it was trying to send. After 3 months of reflection, I have decided that this is bullshit. In fact, I have decided that this book is bullshit.<br/><br/>A 1980s journey into sex, drugs and nihilism, this book builds a </span>
  <span id="freeText16730812786304368064" style="display:none"><b>Honestly, I didn't like it. There were very few redeeming qualities, and I am ready to die on this hill.</b><br /><br />For a while, I was going to leave this review blank and pretend that I was more neutral about it. I was worried that people would tell me that I "didn't get" the book, or the messages that it was trying to send. After 3 months of reflection, I have decided that this is bullshit. In fact, I have decided that this book is bullshit.<br /><br />A 1980s journey into sex, drugs and nihilism, this book builds a convincingly dark and spaced-out atmosphere. At first, I thought that this was going to be a fabulous book. I love books with truly despicable characters (The Catcher in the Rye is my boi), so I had no issue with Clay or his spoilt, rich friends.<br /><br />However, both pretentious and clunky, this book is A LOT LESS INTELLIGENT THAN IT THINKS IT IS. Whilst it tries to raise some interesting points, it lacks so severely in subtlety and sophistication that it reads as Brett Easton Ellis <b>BEGGING</b> you to tell him that he's very clever. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>TLDR: this book can get in the bin.</i>
</b><br />------------------------------------------------<br /><s>Perhaps 2.5 stars? However, the half star may be dropped for how pretentious this book is.<br /><b>I have no idea how to process the second half of this book...</b><br /><i>This book also thinks that it's a lot more intelligent than it actually is.</i><br />RTC.</s></span>

<div id="review_1270775" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1270775" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84507-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1179250595p2/84507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1270775?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/84507-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1270775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6791841571277037066"><b>One question before we start, "Anthracite?"</b><br/><br/><i>Less than Zero</i> is a meditation on the soul-less, physically obsessed world that was born in the 1980s. Yes, perhaps the pedulum has swung to and fro since the publication, but I find the relevance striking to today's pop-culture aesthetic. If Easton Ellis was writing this story today, which his website says he is working on a sequel!?! TECHNOLOGY would or will seperate the characters even more. The Internet is the most convenient place at this time to "</span>
  <span id="freeText6791841571277037066" style="display:none"><b>One question before we start, "Anthracite?"</b><br /><br /><i>Less than Zero</i> is a meditation on the soul-less, physically obsessed world that was born in the 1980s. Yes, perhaps the pedulum has swung to and fro since the publication, but I find the relevance striking to today's pop-culture aesthetic. If Easton Ellis was writing this story today, which his website says he is working on a sequel!?! TECHNOLOGY would or will seperate the characters even more. The Internet is the most convenient place at this time to "Disappear here." One could deconstruct the novel in how we look at Web 2.0; people afraid to merge, full of amoral pornography, hollow identities easily maintained. Look at any comment list on mySpace.com, it would read exactly like any conversation in <i>Less than Zero</i>! Even the title comes from an Elvis Costello lyric where he also sings, "Let's talk about the future, now we've put the past away."<br /><br />Like Sarah mentioned at the book club, Easton Ellis doesn't blatantly show Clay making the last step into (I guess) "empathetic-human-mode." But somehow I would like to think that Clay has moved on, after he left. So matter-of-factly, he didn't disappear.<br /><br />I absolutely adored this book because it made me think, and that is possibly the best reason to like a book. It made me explore new avenues, and it made me realize that I also need to stop and smell the flowers once in a while.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6791841571277037066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2187819938" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2187819938" />
    <a title="Neil Walker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66800248-neil-walker"><img alt="Neil Walker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598790232p2/66800248.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil Walker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil Walker" href="/user/show/66800248-neil-walker">Neil Walker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2187819938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer788354331085547408">Bret Easton Ellis is listed on my author page as of my four biggest influences as a writer, the other three being Chuck Palahniuk, Stephen King and William Shakespeare.<br/><br/>In Less Than Zero, he is writing about his favourite time period, the 1980s, and his favourite location, Los Angeles.<br/><br/>The way he captures the mindset of a certain element of society in the 1980s in a particular place and pushes it to it’s logical conclusion is very much something I was trying to emulate in Drug Gang, with my chosen</span>
  <span id="freeText788354331085547408" style="display:none">Bret Easton Ellis is listed on my author page as of my four biggest influences as a writer, the other three being Chuck Palahniuk, Stephen King and William Shakespeare.<br /><br />In Less Than Zero, he is writing about his favourite time period, the 1980s, and his favourite location, Los Angeles.<br /><br />The way he captures the mindset of a certain element of society in the 1980s in a particular place and pushes it to it’s logical conclusion is very much something I was trying to emulate in Drug Gang, with my chosen time period being the early 2000s and my chosen location being Manchester.<br /><br />Less Than Zero is an incredible debut novel and a nihilistic masterpiece. </span>
  <a data-text-id="788354331085547408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_449885656" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/449885656" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/449885656?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=reading-through-history">reading-through-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer449885656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15909872099349052255">The defense I see most often of Ellis is: "You just don't get the joke." And could there be a more annoying defense? How can you even respond to that? It's meaningless.<br/><br/>And it's not a joke. It's satire; that's totally different.<br/><br/>I spent tonight arguing about Ellis with some very smart contrarians, and here's what they said: Ellis has captured the soulless Me First Generation, and their failure to connect with life, in a really effective way. He refuses his rival David Foster Wallace's edict that l</span>
  <span id="freeText15909872099349052255" style="display:none">The defense I see most often of Ellis is: "You just don't get the joke." And could there be a more annoying defense? How can you even respond to that? It's meaningless.<br /><br />And it's not a joke. It's satire; that's totally different.<br /><br />I spent tonight arguing about Ellis with some very smart contrarians, and here's what they said: Ellis has captured the soulless Me First Generation, and their failure to connect with life, in a really effective way. He refuses his rival David Foster Wallace's edict that literature has to solve something; he insists, with merciless implacability, on simply showing it to you. No solutions, no conclusions.<br /><br />They're right, and that's not valueless. Ellis has achieved something. I actually know these people - not Ellis' caricatures of them, but the real people - and I see what he's describing.<br /><br />The only problem is here's the first sentence of this book: "People are afraid to merge on freeways in Los Angeles." This is a metaphor, I happen to know because I was an English major, and it's fucking stupid. And it's his big theme! This! People are afraid to merge! Like he's discovered some grand truth! He'll return to it like fifty times! Ellis isn't our generation's Henry Miller - he's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/106227-only-connect" title="Only connect!" rel="nofollow noopener">EM Forster.</a><br /><br />So. It's not a useless book. It's a decent satire of shallow pop culture sociopathy. Like Wallace, Ellis is concerned with connection: he wants us to engage with life. (To "merge," even!) Unlike Wallace, he refuses to make helpful suggestions; if you're irritated by Wallace's desperately wide-eyed sincerity, Ellis might speak to you.<br /><br /> But for fuck's sake, it is all awfully tedious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15909872099349052255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1970300490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1970300490" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1970300490?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330539326" href="/book/show/10317124-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1970300490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5676459678591317706">A young student called Clay returns to Los Angeles for Christmas break to see friends and family. His visit reads something like this: “We’re rich kids in LA! Let’s do drugs and have sex – we’re soooo hedonistic and transgressive! Ooo, let’s have sex again and do MORE drugs!” Repeat for 200 pages and you’ve got Bret Easton Ellis’ debut novel Less Than Zero!<br/><br/>Ellis can write really well so it’s a shame he doesn’t really have anything to say besides: rich LA brats are aimless, lost youth, their pare</span>
  <span id="freeText5676459678591317706" style="display:none">A young student called Clay returns to Los Angeles for Christmas break to see friends and family. His visit reads something like this: “We’re rich kids in LA! Let’s do drugs and have sex – we’re soooo hedonistic and transgressive! Ooo, let’s have sex again and do MORE drugs!” Repeat for 200 pages and you’ve got Bret Easton Ellis’ debut novel Less Than Zero!<br /><br />Ellis can write really well so it’s a shame he doesn’t really have anything to say besides: rich LA brats are aimless, lost youth, their parents are drugged-out zombies, and LA culture is surface-level garbage and miserable. Ok – and? He just goes round and round repeating the same blasé party scenes over and over to underline this unimpressive point. <br /><br />All of the vapid one-dimensional “characters” sound the same which might either be a lack of skill on Ellis’ part (he was 21 when this was published) or deliberate to emphasise their lack of character and superficiality. Either way, it doesn’t add up to a very interesting read. At no point did I care about any of them or their depressing lives. <br /><br />It also feels like Ellis is trying too hard to shock. One melodramatic scene focuses on a girl injecting heroin, there’s a male prostitute working off his debt to a scuzzy dealer/pimp, a young teen girl is gang-raped, and everyone’s doing blow all the time. The shock shtick is all this novel has: it’s morbidly interesting but completely without substance and pointless. The same critique could be made of American Psycho but Less Than Zero isn’t as boring. It might’ve worked on ‘80s/early ‘90s audiences but Ellis’ shock tactics read today as really lame and try-hard, like a sad old edgelord’s scribblings. <br /><br />I like Ellis’ writing but mostly for his later books like The Informers, Imperial Bedrooms and, easily my favourite, Lunar Park. His early books like Less Than Zero and American Psycho feel like a writer with enormous talent distracted with being edgy and cool – a one-note author repeating himself ad infinitum rather than one who knows how to develop a theme more roundly and compellingly. But I suppose a lot of that has to do with him being in his twenties when he wrote these too. I highly recommend checking out Lunar Park instead of the dated and tedious Less Than Zero. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5676459678591317706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2248711126" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2248711126" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2248711126?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330539326" href="/book/show/10317124-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=american-canadian">american-canadian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2248711126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13280696714928811075">This is a disquieting, scary book. It deals with youths whose minds are eroded by death of affection, amorality and a devastating spiritual apathy: eighteen-year old kids driving Porsches and vegetating on poolsides, relentlessly abused by the adults around them: absent fathers, lethargic mothers, middle-aged guys lusting after their son's schoolmates; youths exploited and exploiting each other under the dazzling sun of L.A.<br/><br/>Clay, the narrator, is barely conscious of the void he's floating in. Al</span>
  <span id="freeText13280696714928811075" style="display:none">This is a disquieting, scary book. It deals with youths whose minds are eroded by death of affection, amorality and a devastating spiritual apathy: eighteen-year old kids driving Porsches and vegetating on poolsides, relentlessly abused by the adults around them: absent fathers, lethargic mothers, middle-aged guys lusting after their son's schoolmates; youths exploited and exploiting each other under the dazzling sun of L.A.<br /><br />Clay, the narrator, is barely conscious of the void he's floating in. Aloof, emotionless, he describes a daily routine of cocaine, sex, shallow relationships, nervous breakdowns, drugs, self-abasement. Kids with too much money, too much time to kill, and nothing to lose. Total excess and total emptiness at the same time. <br />His friend Julian is possibly the most iconic character of the book: a heroin addict, he starts whoring and selling junk to junior high kids to pay a huge drug debt. No feelings, no emotions, no sense of shame ever come to surface in this downward spiral of glamour and abomination. In Clay's own words, all they really want is <br /><br />"...to see if things like this can actually happen (...) I realize that the money doesn't matter. That all that does is that I want to see the worst."<br /><br />Gang-rapes, snuff movies, prostitution, cruelty: why not, these young boys and girls seem to ask the reader. <br />Why not, indeed? What's supposed to hold them back?<br />Compassion? Respect? Self-esteem? <br />All that matters is to keep going. To see, to take part in it, but not to feel. Because<br /><br />"I don't want to care. If I care about things, it'll just be worse, it'll just be another thing to worry about. It's less painful if I don't care."<br /><br />It's hard to care anyway, when your father wears a Stetson and regularly undergoes face-lifting; when your mother is a self-loathing dumb cow; when your fifteen-year old sisters ask if your school mate is on sale. After all, as Clay's girlfriend points out: <br /><br />"It's hard to feel sorry for someone who doesn't care".<br /><br />No catharsis is therefore to be expected. These boys and girls don't even know what 'catharsis' means, in the first place. <br /><br />"Less Than Zero" is Ellis' debut novel, written when he was only 21. It's also his unparalleled masterpiece to date. <br />No doubt it would be easy to underrate Ellis, or Minimalism in general for that matter; in fact most readers don't seem to get how deep this apparently cold writing style can be. What those brilliant reviewers pretend not to notice is how skilfully, effortlessly Ellis writes - especially the dialogues: realistic, hysterical, desperate. People talking about Porsches, nightclubs, anorexic girls and ODs with the same terrifying indifference. No other contemporary novelist has ever had such a grip on the Zeitgeist, such a surgical precision in depicting the dead soul of an era. Not even J. McInerney ('Bright Lights, Big City'), whose works are highly praised though quite inferior in quality and depth. <br />But then again, Ellis wrote about the world in which he himself had grown: the pool parties on the hill of Los Angeles, the City of Illusion, where dreams -sooner or later- always turn into nightmares.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13280696714928811075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1379732286" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1379732286" />
    <a title="Sean Blake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25471005-sean-blake"><img alt="Sean Blake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528978476p2/25471005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1379732286?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Blake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Blake" href="/user/show/25471005-sean-blake">Sean Blake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330519045" href="/book/show/8473290-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25471005-sean-blake?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1379732286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13655500568680120722"><i>
  <b>Disappear here...</b>
</i><br/><br/>In modern times, it’s hard to imagine a better published first novel than Bret Easton Ellis’ <i>Less Than Zero</i>, published in 1985, when the writer was just 21. The tender age didn’t seem to bother Ellis as he effortlessly deconstructs the youth of his generation in Los Angeles. It’s cold, nihilistic, raw and driven by emotionless desires. It’s this detachedness that gives power to Bret Easton Ellis’ minimalist prose.<br/><br/>Tightly controlled, the novel follows the narrator, Clay, an e</span>
  <span id="freeText13655500568680120722" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>Disappear here...</b>
</i><br /><br />In modern times, it’s hard to imagine a better published first novel than Bret Easton Ellis’ <i>Less Than Zero</i>, published in 1985, when the writer was just 21. The tender age didn’t seem to bother Ellis as he effortlessly deconstructs the youth of his generation in Los Angeles. It’s cold, nihilistic, raw and driven by emotionless desires. It’s this detachedness that gives power to Bret Easton Ellis’ minimalist prose.<br /><br />Tightly controlled, the novel follows the narrator, Clay, an eighteen year old returning to L.A. for Christmas and his drug-fuelled lifestyle and boring existence. But is the novel boring? Far from it. <i>Less Than Zero</i> depicts a young generation that is as true today as it was when released in the eighties. These young adults are morally empty, reckless, uncaring, devoid of emotion and bored by the constraints of societal ethics. Its realism is prevalent in the narrator’s detachment from the world, a detachment we all recognise, understand and relate to, but never on a conscious level; and in novel after novel Ellis paints this bleakly truthful picture of society addicted to blandness and materialism, hopelessly detached from the beautiful world around them.* We see these people all around us, and Bret Easton Ellis is simply showing the reader.<br /><br />A lonely, oppressive and disturbing atmosphere of truthfulness dominates the novel, even in the simplest scenes, and simply doesn’t let go until the last page. <i>Less Than Zero</i> is a beautifully controlled and impressive first novel from a brilliant observer and satirist of the Western world.<br /><br /><br /><i>* — Cognitive dissonance perhaps?</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13655500568680120722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/705256969" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/705256969?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2013">2013</a>
