        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer705256969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9935438042728534916"><i>“Fear never shows up and the party ends early.”</i> <br/>― Bret Easton Ellis, Less Than Zero<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463466531i/19119985._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='347' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I'm afraid I OD'd on LA novels this week. Started with 'Less than Zero', added the Black Dahlia, and finished with The Day of the Locusts. Let me just say, I'm definitely not planning on moving to that City where people and their dreams both go to die. A visit of 3 days was just enough to reestablished my conviction.<br/><br/>I had a hard time deciding whether to read Less than Zero. I hold B.E.E. with a certain level of co</span>
  <span id="freeText9935438042728534916" style="display:none"><i>“Fear never shows up and the party ends early.”</i> <br>― Bret Easton Ellis, Less Than Zero<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463466531i/19119985._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="347" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'm afraid I OD'd on LA novels this week. Started with 'Less than Zero', added the Black Dahlia, and finished with The Day of the Locusts. Let me just say, I'm definitely not planning on moving to that City where people and their dreams both go to die. A visit of 3 days was just enough to reestablished my conviction.<br><br>I had a hard time deciding whether to read Less than Zero. I hold B.E.E. with a certain level of contempt. My feelings about him are similar to Norman Mailer's:<br><br><i>"How one wishes this writer was without talent!"</i> <br><br>I would only add, how I personally wish this writer was without a Twitter account. I debate in my mind if I could, with a switch, delete all of Twitter I think I would just to eliminate B.E.E.'s <a href="https://twitter.com/BretEastonEllis/status/244359661950754816" rel="nofollow noopener">toxic presence</a> there. Which is I guess throws me firmly into the Franzen <a href="http://www.newrepublic.com/article/114762/jennifer-weiner-responds-jonathan-franzen" rel="nofollow noopener">camp</a>. But ye GODs really Franzen v B.E.E. Both of those together is like slamming two plutonium bowls together. They both kinda make my head want to explode.<br><br>Anyway, 'Less than Zero' is sterile and precise in both language and style, but like David Lynch movies (I'm thinking of Blue Velvet and Fire Walk with Me) underneath those well-trimmed nails is the flesh and stink of corruption. It is like Lord of the Flies, but the Island is LA, and fuck the conch cause here there is plenty of sunshine, sex, drugs. The book had a lot of potential, but when I compare it with other early, debut novels filled with elements of angst and school like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Catcher in the Rye</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46165.This_Side_of_Paradise" title="This Side of Paradise by F. Scott Fitzgerald" rel="nofollow noopener">This Side of Paradise</a>, or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2287.Other_Voices__Other_Rooms" title="Other Voices, Other Rooms by Truman Capote" rel="nofollow noopener">Other Voices, Other Rooms</a>, it just seems flat and when not flat indulgent and uneven. But, it was a bit shocking and had a definite voice, so there is that. But in the end, final analysis, I think it was more zeitgeist than literature. More pulp than fiction.<br><br>I tend to tread lightly when it comes to BEE, just because while I sometimes approve of what the beast brings, I don't always want to live with the idea that I fed that glossy LA beast. <br><br><br><br> <br><br><br> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9935438042728534916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_799711134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/799711134" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605586833p2/869064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/799711134?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer799711134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16909089673927135913">I've never read Ellis before, and since he published this when he was just 21, I'm not sure if or how to really come to grips with it. The style is obviously super flat, though whether this is because Ellis simply wasn't able to write otherwise at such a young age or if he was just smart enough to realize he's probably too young to try, I can't say. But I found Clay's cool, detached narration to be, if nothing else, fairly engaging. Not revelatory, not brilliant, but interesting enough to keep m</span>
  <span id="freeText16909089673927135913" style="display:none">I've never read Ellis before, and since he published this when he was just 21, I'm not sure if or how to really come to grips with it. The style is obviously super flat, though whether this is because Ellis simply wasn't able to write otherwise at such a young age or if he was just smart enough to realize he's probably too young to try, I can't say. But I found Clay's cool, detached narration to be, if nothing else, fairly engaging. Not revelatory, not brilliant, but interesting enough to keep me reading.<br /><br />This book is, knowingly or not, basically like throwing acid in the face of Reagan's America. Trust fund brats in the San Fernando Valley with too much money, too much cocaine, too much apathetic sex. They go to cool parties and eat at 3 star restaurants the way other people have to wake up to work the early shift and cut coupons. <br /><br />The self-rightous midwesterner in me thinks they just need some "structure." The bitter nihilist in me wants to either cheer them on or shoot them in the face. The slacker millenial in me shrugs and suspects that this is probably how it's always been for louche rich kids. Kind of like Public Enemy or early Prince, the transgressive punch this once had is kind of diluted with time, I can imagine a modern middle aged housewife reading this in 2013 and being disgusted, but I can't imagine her being totally caught off guard and utterly horrified like she might have been in the mid 80's. The simple fact is that the rarified world of nihilistic anxiety that Clay occupies is much more widespread in our day and age (come on, who doesn't have HBO, a DVD player and ample access to booze and drugs in 2013?) than it was in 1985, even if the wealth that powers it remains as exclusive as it ever did.<br /><br />Maybe that means that Bret Easton Ellis, at only 21 years old, basically got it right the first time around and has only become more right about the way we live in the decades since. Maybe this book where people move zombie like from one empty pleasure on to another to another just can't be shocking because what's really shocking, or unrelatable in this book aside from a few moments of casual sexual slavery (and even those, sadly, aren't a huge imaginative stretch)? Whether you "like" Less than Zero or not, it's kind of impossible to deny that it's view of our reality is more widespread and more comprehensible now than it was almost 30 years ago. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16909089673927135913" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A.C. Lillywhite" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A.C. Lillywhite" href="/user/show/1307091-a-c-lillywhite">A.C. Lillywhite</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one I currently care for.</span></div>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to A.C. by: </span>
      Tatum

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33206545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2726186807393014914">This book probably deserved more than three stars. But I just can't give it any more than that. I HATE this book. I hate it with my whole soul. It's so true and I am massively depressed after reading it. It perfectly illustrates the life of a completely useless waste of a human being and all his useless friends and their useless lives. It's awful. They should all be put out of their (and our) misery. The best thing I can say is that this book serves as a glorious example of how not to be. The sc</span>
  <span id="freeText2726186807393014914" style="display:none">This book probably deserved more than three stars. But I just can't give it any more than that. I HATE this book. I hate it with my whole soul. It's so true and I am massively depressed after reading it. It perfectly illustrates the life of a completely useless waste of a human being and all his useless friends and their useless lives. It's awful. They should all be put out of their (and our) misery. The best thing I can say is that this book serves as a glorious example of how not to be. The scary thing is that's it's probably a pretty accurate portrayal of a certain type of people.<br /><br />If I had the choice, I would have put it down after I finished the first twenty pages and wanted to shoot myself, but I had to finish it for a class. I would not recommend this to anyone who is already depressed. There is a slight risk of becoming suicidal. I would also not recommend this to anyone who is currently blissfully happy. You should enjoy that while you can.<br /><br />I can say that this is very well written. I typically cannot stand first-person present tense. It's like running when you could walk along leisurely, but it's not so bad when it's a quick read like this. Ellis makes an incredibly good point about the shallow lives that some people live through. Also, it did help me appreciate my own life a lot. I feel like a really good person now, because I'm nothing like these creeps, which is all you can hope for in life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2726186807393014914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_21376131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21376131" />
    <a title="Ricky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/127810-ricky"><img alt="Ricky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514210847p2/127810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21376131?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ricky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ricky" href="/user/show/127810-ricky">Ricky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">nihilists, dummies</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/127810-ricky?shelf=saw-the-film">saw-the-film</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21376131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1480909237078595048">Okay, so I was willing to accept this book as a criticism of the emptiness of modern culture. I was willing to overlook the dullness and amateurishness. But it just got duller and duller and duller. And yes, we know American culture is a wasteland. But there has to be a more interesting way to get this across. And if I am to accept this book as metaphor, I'm going to have to disagree with its premise because I think it's cynical to the point of inaccuracy. It was like a Wes Anderson movie: I can</span>
  <span id="freeText1480909237078595048" style="display:none">Okay, so I was willing to accept this book as a criticism of the emptiness of modern culture. I was willing to overlook the dullness and amateurishness. But it just got duller and duller and duller. And yes, we know American culture is a wasteland. But there has to be a more interesting way to get this across. And if I am to accept this book as metaphor, I'm going to have to disagree with its premise because I think it's cynical to the point of inaccuracy. It was like a Wes Anderson movie: I can only take so much "art" centered around the neuroses of wealthy assholes. <br /><br />I appreciated the bit about how when the old lady fell down or whatever and all these people outside La Scala attended to her and an ambulance came and nobody inside the restaurant gave it any notice. I thought that worked. And the crazy homeless lady squatting on a sidewalk by the freeway. But, just as I was beginning to appreciate these details a voice in my head reminded me that my time would have been better spent reading stories about those characters.<br /><br />I'm sure Ellis was being critical of his milieu but much of it comes across as a sort of earnest reveling. <br /><br />There were a few things I liked here but mostly my response is a mixture or boredom and "barf!"</span>
  <a data-text-id="1480909237078595048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1455579929" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455579929" />
    <a title="The Bibliophagist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12885107-the-bibliophagist"><img alt="The Bibliophagist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453418567p2/12885107.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1455579929?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Bibliophagist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Bibliophagist" href="/user/show/12885107-the-bibliophagist">The Bibliophagist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330519045" href="/book/show/8473290-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12885107-the-bibliophagist?shelf=never-again">never-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12885107-the-bibliophagist?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12885107-the-bibliophagist?shelf=annoying-characters">annoying-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12885107-the-bibliophagist?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12885107-the-bibliophagist?shelf=physically-own">physically-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12885107-the-bibliophagist?shelf=male-pov">male-pov</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1455579929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12235278366003020754">I would give this book Less Than Zero stars if I could.<br/><br/>I picked up this book for multiple reasons. But the main reason was because it was in the bargain bin at Books-A-Million for $5.97. Mr. Ellis was my age when this book was published, so I thought I’d get great insight on troubled young adults against the back drop of Los Angeles, but instead I got a bunch of obnoxious teenagers with drug problems. <br/><br/><b> Here's what's wrong:</b><br/><br/><b> Writing Style </b><br/>One of the main issues I had with this book was the writing</span>
  <span id="freeText12235278366003020754" style="display:none">I would give this book Less Than Zero stars if I could.<br /><br />I picked up this book for multiple reasons. But the main reason was because it was in the bargain bin at Books-A-Million for $5.97. Mr. Ellis was my age when this book was published, so I thought I’d get great insight on troubled young adults against the back drop of Los Angeles, but instead I got a bunch of obnoxious teenagers with drug problems. <br /><br /><b> Here's what's wrong:</b><br /><br /><b> Writing Style </b><br />One of the main issues I had with this book was the writing style. The writing was cringe-worthy at best, and downright atrocious the rest of the time. I literally felt like I was reading a story written by a fourth grader. A fourth grader with an obsession of drugs and alcohol, but a fourth grader nonetheless. 80% of this book was telling, not showing. The scenes were never fleshed out, every other paragraph was a new scene, jumping from place to place. Not only that, the sentences were all run-ons! I think one page (or at least most of it) was an entire sentence! And when Mr. Ellis finally did decide to start a new sentence, it was always with “And.” And and and.<br /><br />Also, there were so many characters in the book. Almost too many to keep up with. Clay, Spin, Trent, Blair, Jared, Julian, Rip, Daniel, Finn, Kim, Muriel – just the ones off the top of my head. This wouldn't be issue if these characters were well developed, but since all were basically just given was a name, it was just hard to follow. Another fun fact is that we’re told any character descriptions. Not even a little detail like hair or eye color. They were all just names on a page.<br /><br />(<i>“Overall, I was pretty shocked. It was pretty good writing for someone who was 19. I was pretty surprised by the level of writing."</i> – Mr. Ellis after reading Less Than Zero 20 years later. <br /><br />Are you kidding me, Mr. Ellis? I would be horrified if I wrote and published this! I thought time would change you, make you realize the error of your ways!)<br /> <br /><b> The narrator </b><br />Another issue I had with this book was our main character, Clay. Clay was devoid of any emotion or feeling. He simply existed. Telling us his story in the blandest way possible. We never got into his head or his heart. Having a character so detached from his own story was entirely draining to read about. Mr. Ellis could have delved into Clay’s sexuality and his family, but instead he just skimmed the surface. We were never given any insight to the characters. We had no reasoning for what drove them or why the behaved like they did. <br /><br /><b> There's no plotline...</b><br />There was no substance to this book. No plot. I kept waiting for something to happen, but nothing ever did. There was no goal set for our character, no reason for me to root for him. I just don’t see how an entire story could have absolutely no direction. <br /><br />-----<br /><br />I had to force myself to finish this “novel” because of the bland characters, awful writing, and lack of plot. If you like whiny books about self-absorbed rich kids, then look no further. I could see how this book would get published in the eighties (just mention coke and sex enough and it’s iconic), but nowadays I just don’t see the appeal. The shock factor wears off twenty pages in. <br /><br />I get it, the rich kids of LA are spoiled, shallow addicts. Moving on.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12235278366003020754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_273731715" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273731715" />
    <a title="Clare" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4444679-clare"><img alt="Clare" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452634477p2/4444679.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273731715?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clare" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clare" href="/user/show/4444679-clare">Clare</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330539326" href="/book/show/10317124-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4444679-clare?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4444679-clare?shelf=christmas">christmas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273731715" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5741836551657415795">A large part of this book is boring and the characters are all just horrible people but the overall effect is amazing. It races along full of boring details and you feel like sticking pins in your eyes and shouting at the characters but then it drops in anvil heavy, horrific statements so subtly it just merges into the text. It's so subtle it makes it all the more shocking.<br/>Bret Easton Ellis is amzing at writing dialogue. This was a point that kept me going. All his "and then I did this, and then</span>
  <span id="freeText5741836551657415795" style="display:none">A large part of this book is boring and the characters are all just horrible people but the overall effect is amazing. It races along full of boring details and you feel like sticking pins in your eyes and shouting at the characters but then it drops in anvil heavy, horrific statements so subtly it just merges into the text. It's so subtle it makes it all the more shocking.<br />Bret Easton Ellis is amzing at writing dialogue. This was a point that kept me going. All his "and then I did this, and then I did this, blah blah blah" is punctuated by perfect and real dialogue. Also I like the reminiscing parts.<br />The last paragraph makes you think "wow!" and it all comes together in string of horrible, boring and growing ideas. A little book that makes lots of big points about lots of everyday things.<br /><br />-----------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />Updated - Full Review as on Citizen's Eye Website<br /><br />I read Less Than Zero in one night, about four and a half hours and for most of it I was bored senseless. Every twenty pages or so I’d dramatically let the book fall to my knee; finger still holding my place, and roll my eyes thinking “When will something happen?!”<br /><br />But then things start to happen and you quieten down. And then you reach the end and you think “Wow.” And all the boring stuff doesn’t seem quite so boring, it all had a place and wrote its place so well.<br /><br />Anyhow, Less Than Zero is the story of Clay and his friends being bored in Los Angeles with too much money and no real direction. They are generally all hateful people which makes the boringness even harder to bear. You do start to feel like sticking pins in your eyes as Clay describes his days lying about, his nights pushing drugs in his face and cruising around in expensive cars and going to dull sounding parties. These people aren’t real friends, they’re bored scene people who have rich and famous parents and can’t be bothered to pick up their own lives in a constructive way. <br /><br />But this is how it feels, to be a character in the book I mean and that’s why it is so effective. You become desperate for something to happen and then when it does, dropping anvil like statements subtly into the story, you are a little relieved but mainly repelled and you just want to turn away from this whole world. <br /><br />The dialogue keeps you going really. Bret Easton Ellis is just so good at writing dialogue, it crackles into life like an old record player, the more you read their voices the clearer they become and more real. <br /><br />The text is interspersed with passages of reminiscence from Clay. These are filled with some truly terrific writing; contrasted with the dull but effective narration and the sparkling dialogue we get a full picture of the extent of this writer’s talent. Now we have a built up portrait of what this book achieves and really it is great. You think it is boring but it’s not, it’s great and when you can see the whole thing you see what I mean and the last sentence really hammers home the true visionary nature of this book. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5741836551657415795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2383127551" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2383127551" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4857864-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592769210p2/4857864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2383127551?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4857864-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/26822421-less-than-zero">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2383127551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10325246074774059084">Less Than Zero describes the lives of wealthy, jaded teenagers in LA during the 80’s, the age of greed and consumerism (cue Gordon Gekko’s famous speech in 1987’s Wall Street).<br/><br/>All characters walk around in a nihilistic, monochromatic world: continually smoking, blowing coke up their nose, taking prescription medicine and having loads and loads of sex. But they are all in a grey, unfeeling hell of their own making.<br/><br/>It’s hard to differentiate each character in this book, because they are all the sa</span>
  <span id="freeText10325246074774059084" style="display:none">Less Than Zero describes the lives of wealthy, jaded teenagers in LA during the 80’s, the age of greed and consumerism (cue Gordon Gekko’s famous speech in 1987’s Wall Street).<br /><br />All characters walk around in a nihilistic, monochromatic world: continually smoking, blowing coke up their nose, taking prescription medicine and having loads and loads of sex. But they are all in a grey, unfeeling hell of their own making.<br /><br />It’s hard to differentiate each character in this book, because they are all the same. Even the main character, Clay, our focal point of this fugue state, moves restlessly through this world, shifting and shuffling around in a passive existence. At the start of the novel, there’s a running joke of how his trip out east to attend school in New Hampshire has left him without his California tan. But the constant references to disappearance and ghosts lead us to believe it is more than a tan the author is talking about.<br /><br />This novel is slick and silver for the majority of its content, but closes in for the kill at the end, when things get harsh and uncomfortable. It’s quite a cute trick, the author lulls us into a state of lethargy like it’s characters, but then reveals an even seamier world that lies underneath. It’s as shocking to us as it is to Clay.<br /><br />Less Than Zero is not as good as Easton’s other more famous 80’s novel on consumerism, American Psycho, which counterbalances the shallow world of Patrick Bateman’s existence with psychotic sprees of malevolent violence. To be honest, Less Than Zero is quite boring at times, which maybe exactly what the author intended. Still it’s an interesting book, despite it’s shortcomings, and was written by the author at the tender age of 21 (being facetious here: there ain't nothing tender about this author).</span>
  <a data-text-id="10325246074774059084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BcXb9EPffiA22cVKRI9HSUXVB834eEXk/4GBVfr116FUskFY9sYh9imklR0geFXHFMibVRVskDvqy+tV2rd7vA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WlIZJl7wnlYKWn5zxtCtHO+9usi8mruiiYFBeLx3z9cLJYOK6+nBgBUnLiSiJ7+SvqAmUFGObn2cyyt4nDVjyg==')})
  }

When his family starts to fracture, Mark\'s life swings out of control and he succumbs to the defeatism which has taken hold in Edinburgh\'s grimmer areas. The way out is heroin.\n\nIt\'s no better for his friends. Spud Murphy is paid off from his job, Tommy Lawrence feels himself being sucked into a life of petty crime and violence - the worlds of the thieving Matty Connell and psychotic Franco Begbie. Only Sick Boy, the supreme manipulator of the opposite sex, seems to ride the current, scamming and hustling his way through it all.\n\nSkagboys charts their journey from likely lads to young men addicted to the heroin which has flooded their disintegrating community. This is the 1980s: a time of drugs, poverty, AIDS, violence, political strife and hatred - but a lot of laughs, and maybe just a little love; a decade which changed Britain for ever. Yet there are moments of exquisite tenderness in these troubled lives. Georgette, the transvestite who falls in love with a callous hoodlum; Tralala, the conniving prostitute who plumbs the depths of sexual degradation; and Harry, the strike leader who hides his true desires behind a boorish masculinity, are unforgettable creations. Last Exit to Brooklyn was banned by British courts in 1967, a decision that was reversed the following year with the help of a number of writers and critics including Anthony Burgess and Frank Kermode.\n\nHubert Selby, Jr. (1928-2004) was born in Brooklyn, New York. At the age of 15, he dropped out of school and went to sea with the merchant marines. While at sea he was diagnosed with lung disease. With no other way to make a living, he decided to try writing: \'I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer.\' In 1964 he completed his first book, Last Exit to Brooklyn, which has since become a cult classic. In 1966, it was the subject of an obscenity trial in the UK. His other books include The Room, The Demon, Requiem for a Dream, The Willow Tree and Waiting Period. In 2000, Requiem for a Dream was adapted into a film starring Jared Leto and Ellen Burstyn, and directed by Darren Aronofsky.\n\n\'Last Exit to Brooklyn will explode like a rusty hellish bombshell <div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2751.Bret_Easton_Ellis"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1405340867p3/2751.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12874930134349816251">Bret Easton Ellis is an American author. He is considered to be one of the major Generation X authors and was regarded as one of the so-called literary Brat Pack, which also included <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58272.Tama_Janowitz' title='Tama Janowitz' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tama Janowitz</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14079.Jay_McInerney' title='Jay McInerney' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jay McInerney</a>. He has called himself a moralist, although he has often been pegged as a nihilist. His characters are generally young vacuous people, who are aware of their depravity but choose to enj</span>
  <span id="freeText12874930134349816251" style="display:none">Bret Easton Ellis is an American author. He is considered to be one of the major Generation X authors and was regarded as one of the so-called literary Brat Pack, which also included <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58272.Tama_Janowitz" title="Tama Janowitz" rel="nofollow noopener">Tama Janowitz</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14079.Jay_McInerney" title="Jay McInerney" rel="nofollow noopener">Jay McInerney</a>. He has called himself a moralist, although he has often been pegged as a nihilist. His characters are generally young vacuous people, who are aware of their depravity but choose to enjoy it. The novels are also linked by common, recurring characters, and dystopic locales (such as Los Angeles and New York).</span>
  <a data-text-id="12874930134349816251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
